Why 'Nabi' Was Chosen Over 'Rasul'
Author explains why he used 'nabi' instead of 'rasul' and cites Qur'anic support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author deliberately chose 'nabi' over 'rasul' and how this choice underscores the Prophet's dignity and aligns with Qur'anic usage.

Core Idea
Prophethood alone merits blessings, and the Qur'an itself uses 'al-nabi'.

You may wonder why the author used 'al-nabi' rather than 'al-rasul' in this context. This small word choice carries theological weight: it affirms that Muhammad's prophethood is enough to deserve divine and angelic blessings, and it mirrors the Qur'an's own phrasing. The passage gives the author's rationale.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice the conciseness of the argument.
The key phrase is 'بوصف النبوة'—'by virtue of prophethood'.
2
Identify the two reasons
The author gives two reasons: (1) the Prophet deserves blessings by virtue of prophethood just as by messengership, (2) this usage agrees with the Qur'anic verse in Al-Ahzab. Find these in the text.
3
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some might think blessings are only due to messengership. The author clarifies that prophethood alone suffices. Consider why this distinction matters for Islamic theology.
Prophethood is a direct grace from Allah; it is inherently dignified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعبر بالنبي ولم يعبر بالرسول إشارة إلى أنه يستحقُ الصلاة والسلام بوصف النبوة كما يستحقُها بوصف الرسالة، وموافقة لقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ اللَّهَ وَمَلَيْكَتَهُ يُصَلُّونَ عَلَى النَّبي ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٥٦] .
And he expressed it with 'al-nabi' and did not express it with 'al-rasul' as an indication that he deserves prayer and greeting by virtue of prophethood just as he deserves it by virtue of messengership, and in agreement with His statement, the Exalted: 'Indeed, Allah and His angels pray upon the Prophet' [Al-Ahzab: 56].
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage gives the author's reason for choosing the word 'nabi' (prophet) rather than 'rasul' (messenger) in the context of sending blessings. The author states that using 'nabi' indicates that the Prophet (peace be upon him) deserves salawat (blessings and greetings) due to his attribute of prophethood just as he deserves them due to his attribute of messengership. Additionally, this choice aligns with the Qur'anic verse in Surah Al-Ahzab (33:56), which explicitly mentions 'the Prophet' in the command to send blessings upon him.

Key Terms
النبي
الرسول
الصلاة والسلام
آية الأحزاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Blessings are only deserved because of messengership, not prophethood.
Correction: The author states that the Prophet deserves blessings by virtue of prophethood just as by virtue of messengership, and this is supported by the Qur'anic verse which uses 'al-nabi'.

Checkpoint
Why did the author use 'nabi' instead of 'rasul' when discussing blessings?
A) Because he dislikes the word 'rasul'
B) To indicate that prophethood alone merits blessings, and to follow the Qur'anic usage
C) Because the poem only permits 'nabi'
D) To avoid confusion with messengers among angels

Key Takeaway
The author chooses 'nabi' to emphasize that the Prophet deserves blessings by virtue of his prophethood, in harmony with the Qur'an.

Definition of a Companion and the Rule of Apostasy
Definition of 'sahabi' and its conditions, including apostasy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a companion (sahabi) according to the scholars, including the conditions for companionship and the ruling on apostasy and return to faith.

Core Idea
A companion is one who met the Prophet in faith; apostasy severs companionship permanently unless one returns.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the technical meaning of 'companion' (sahabi) as distinct from its linguistic sense. He explains that companionship requires meeting the Prophet with faith after his commission, and addresses what happens if a person apostatizes. This definition is crucial for understanding the status of the Prophet's companions and the implications of apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the passage carefully to grasp the linguistic and technical definitions of 'sahabi'.
Notice the distinction between 'ism jam'' (collective noun) and broken plural.
2
Understand the conditions
Identify the conditions for companionship: meeting the Prophet with faith after prophethood, and that dying upon Islam is a condition for continuity, not origin.
3
Analyze the apostasy ruling
Examine the examples of Abd Allah ibn Khatal and Abd Allah ibn Abi Sarh to see how apostasy affects companionship.
Pay attention to the difference between Shafi'i and Maliki views.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this definition matters for theology and hadith studies.
The definition affects who is included among the companions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وَصَحْبِهِ) خصَّهُم مع دخولهم في الآل بالمعنى الأعم لمزيد الاهتمام والتحقيق أن ( صحباً) ليس جمعاً لـ (صاحب) بل اسم جمع وإن كان له واحد من وهو صاحب ، وهو لغةً : من طالَتْ عشرتك به ، والمراد به هنا الصحابي; وهو مَنْ اجتمع بنبينا مؤمناً به بعد البعثة في محلّ التعارف; بأن يكون على وجه الأرض وإن لم يره، أو لم يرو عنه شيئاً ، أو لم يميز على الصحيح، وأما قولهم: (ومات على الإسلام) فهو شرط لدوام الصحبة ، لا لأصلها (4) ، فإن ارتد والعياذ بالله تعالى ومات مرتدا، فليس بصحابي ; كعبد الله بن خَطل، وأما من عاد للإيمان كعبد الله بن أبي سرح فتعود له الصحبة، لكن مجرَّدة عن الثواب عندنا معاشر الشافعية، واشتهر أنها لا تعود عند المالكية، لكن المصرَّح به في كتبهم التردُّد، وحينئذ فلا مانع. (4) وإلا لما تحققت حال الحياة كما أفاده العلامة الأمير.
His saying: 'and his companions (sahbihi)'. He specified them despite their inclusion in 'al' in the general sense for extra attention. And the verification is that 'sahban' is not a broken plural of 'sahib' but rather a collective noun (ism jam'), even though it has a singular from it which is 'sahib'. And it means linguistically: one whose association with you is long. And what is meant here is the companion (sahabi): he is the one who met our Prophet, believing in him after the commission (ba'thah) in a place of mutual acquaintance; (this includes) being on the face of the earth even if he did not see him, or did not report anything from him, or did not distinguish (lam yumayyiz) according to the correct view. As for their saying: 'and he died upon Islam', it is a condition for the continuity of companionship (dawam al-suhbah), not for its origin (asl). For if he apostatizes, God forbid, and dies as an apostate, he is not a companion, like Abd Allah ibn Khatal. As for one who returns to faith like Abd Allah ibn Abi Sarh, companionship returns to him, but stripped of reward according to us, the Shafi'i community. And it is well-known that it does not return according to the Malikis, but what is explicitly stated in their books is hesitation (taraddud). Therefore, there is no objection. (4) Otherwise it would not be realized during life as al-Allamah al-Amir indicated.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'sahabi' (companion). It begins by noting that the word 'sahbihi' in the poem gives special attention to companions even though they are already included in the general meaning of 'al'. The author clarifies that 'sahban' is not a broken plural of 'sahib' but a collective noun, though it has a singular form. Linguistically, 'sahib' means one with whom you have long association. Here, the technical definition is: anyone who met the Prophet, believing in him after his commission, at a place of mutual acquaintance (i.e., on earth), even if they never saw him, or never narrated from him, or never distinguished themselves, according to the correct view. The condition 'died upon Islam' is not for the origin of companionship but for its continuity; if one apostatizes and dies as an apostate, he is not a companion (e.g., Abd Allah ibn Khatal). If one returns to Islam, companionship is restored but without reward according to the Shafi'is, while the Malikis are reported to deny its return, though their books indicate hesitation. The author concludes that there is no harm in the Shafi'i view. The footnote emphasizes that companionship must be realizable during life.

Key Terms
صحابي
صحب
اسم جمع
صحبة
ردة
عود الصحبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing the Prophet is necessary to be a companion.
Correction: The correct view is that meeting the Prophet (even without seeing him) suffices, as the passage states: 'even if he did not see him'.

Checkpoint
According to the Shafi'i view, what happens to the status of companionship for a person who apostatizes and then returns to Islam?
A) Companionship is lost permanently
B) Companionship is restored with full reward
C) Companionship is restored but without reward
D) Companionship is restored only if they saw the Prophet

Key Takeaway
A companion is defined as a believer who met the Prophet after his commission; apostasy severs companionship permanently unless one returns, whereupon it is restored but without reward according to the Shafi'is.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage teaches that companionship with the Prophet is defined by meeting him in faith, but its continuity requires dying upon Islam. Examine your own spiritual life: is there a specific 'meeting with faith' that you once had—perhaps a moment of conviction or a turning point—that you have allowed to become just a memory without ongoing commitment? What concrete step can you take today to renew that meeting and ensure it leads to a steadfast continuation?
?
Abdullah ibn Abi Sarh returned to faith and his companionship was restored, but without reward. How does this challenge your understanding of repentance? Are there sins or failures in your life that you assume are completely erased upon return, yet perhaps leave a lingering deficiency in your spiritual status? How does this motivate you to guard against even the thought of apostasy (in belief or action) and to cherish every act of obedience as a treasure that cannot be fully reclaimed if lost?
?
The author notes that the condition of 'dying upon Islam' is for continuity, not origin, otherwise companionship would not be realized during life. Apply this logic to a relationship you value: is your commitment to it based solely on the initial connection, or are you actively and daily fulfilling conditions for its continuity? In your relationship with Allah, are you relying on a past moment of faith (like a testimony or a spiritual experience) without nurturing the ongoing condition of steadfastness? What will you do today to strengthen that continuity?

The Legal and Logical Problems with Mukhalafah on Fitrah
Sanusi refutes the use of mukhalafah by pointing out legal and categorical limits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of mukhalafah cannot be used to claim that every child is born Muslim, due to legal and logical issues.

Core Idea
Mukhalafah leads to impossible legal conclusions and non-exhaustive categories.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses an objection: does the hadith 'every child is born in fitrah' imply that every child is born Muslim? He argues that such an interpretation relies on the concept of mukhalafah (opposite meaning), which fails on two counts: it would impose legal rulings on infants that cannot apply, and it assumes only four categories of religion, ignoring modern realities like communism and capitalism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the author's logical steps.
He starts with a conditional statement.
2
Identify the first problem: legal impossibility
Observe that if every newborn is Muslim, then a child who apostatizes would be subject to hadd punishment, but that is not applied to minors.
3
Identify the second problem: non-exhaustive categories
Note that mukhalafah only works if the listed categories cover all possibilities, but they do not account for modern ideologies.
The author gives examples like communism and capitalism.
4
Summarize the two flaws
Combine the legal and categorical arguments to see why mukhalafah is invalid here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً : لا تعارض إلا إذا حملنا الحديث على مفهوم المخالفة، ومفهوم المخالفة لم يتم الاستدلال به؛ لأنه يدل على أن أول ما يولد الإنسان فهو مسلم، وكيف هذا ؟ والطفل دون البلوغ لا يحكم بإسلامه أو كفره إلا إلحاقاً ، فلا تقيم عليه الحد إن ارتد عن الإسلام، ولا نقطع يده إن سرق ما دام دون البلوغ. ولا يتم الاستدلال بمفهوم المخالفة إلا إذا حصرنا المولود بالحالات الأربعة : اليهودية والنصرانية والمجوسية والإسلام والواقع قد يكون غير ذاك كالشيوعية والرأسمالية مثلاً . ولا يصار إلى مفهوم المخالفة ؛ لأنه يوجد رواية لمسلم والبيهقي والحاكم : «فإن كانا مسلمين فمسلم :أي: إن كل مولود إن كان أبواه يهوديين فيهودي، وإن نصرانيين فنصراني، وإن مجوسيين فمجوسي، وإن مُسلمين فمُسلم. وهذا المفهوم خالف نصا نبويًّا آخر وهو : حتى يعرب عنه لسانه، فإن أعرب عنه لسانه فإما شاكراً، وإما كفوراً». فليس يدل ظاهر الحديث ولا منطوقه ولا مفهومه على أن الفطرة هي الإسلام وهي العلم بأمور الدين، بل الصواب أن الفطرة: (لا تعلمون شيئاً) كما دلت عليه الآية .
Secondly: There is no contradiction unless we interpret the hadith according to the concept of opposite meaning (mukhalafah), and the concept of opposite meaning is not used as evidence; because it indicates that when a person is first born he is Muslim, and how is this? The child before puberty is not judged as Muslim or disbeliever except by affiliation, so we do not impose the hadd punishment on him if he apostatizes from Islam, nor do we cut off his hand if he steals as long as he is under puberty. And the concept of opposite meaning is not considered evidence unless we restrict the newborn to the four cases: Judaism, Christianity, Zoroastrianism, and Islam, while reality may be other than that, like communism and capitalism for example. And the concept of opposite meaning is not resorted to because there exists a narration by Muslim, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Hakim: 'If both parents are Muslim, then the child is Muslim' – meaning: every newborn, if his parents are Jews, then he is a Jew; if Christians, then a Christian; if Zoroastrians, then a Zoroastrian; if Muslims, then a Muslim. And this concept contradicts another prophetic text: 'Until his tongue expresses, and if his tongue expresses, then either grateful or ungrateful.' So neither the apparent meaning, nor the expressed meaning, nor the implied meaning of the hadith indicates that fitrah is Islam or knowledge of religious matters. Rather, the correct opinion is that fitrah is: 'You know nothing' as the verse indicates.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a potential objection against the hadith that every child is born in fitrah. It deals with the concept of contrary meaning (mukhalafah) in hadith interpretation. The author argues that one cannot claim the hadith implies that every child is born Muslim by using the principle of mukhalafah, because that principle leads to an impossible legal conclusion: infants cannot be judged as Muslims or disbelievers in a legal sense (e.g., hadd punishments do not apply to minors). Moreover, the principle of mukhalafah only works if the categories are exhaustive, but the mentioned four (Judaism, Christianity, Zoroastrianism, Islam) are not exhaustive in modern times (communism, capitalism). Additionally, there is a direct narration that the child follows the religion of his parents, and another hadith that one's true nature is shown by his speech after maturity. Therefore, the correct interpretation of fitrah is the state of not knowing anything, as stated in the Quran (Surah al-Nahl, 16:78).

Key Terms
مفهوم المخالفة
المولود
يهودي
نصراني
مجوسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith 'every child is born in fitrah' means every child is born a Muslim with full legal consequences.
Correction: The passage shows that this interpretation leads to legal absurdities and does not account for all belief systems.

Checkpoint
Why is the concept of mukhalafah not valid for interpreting the fitrah hadith according to the passage?
A) Because it would mean children are born knowing nothing.
B) Because it would impose legal punishments on minors and only works if categories are exhaustive.
C) Because the hadith is not authentic.
D) Because the Prophet explicitly said children are born Christians.

Key Takeaway
Mukhalafah fails as evidence because it entails legal rulings on minors and assumes an incomplete list of beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged someone's faith based on a label, assuming that the only possibilities are the few you know? How might your attitude change when you realize that God's creation includes many paths and that your judgment may be based on incomplete categories? Will you commit to withholding judgment until you have fuller knowledge?
?
The lesson shows that we cannot treat a child as a Muslim or non-Muslim before they reach maturity. Do you treat children around you as if they already bear full responsibility? Are you demanding from them a level of faith or practice that they are not yet accountable for? How can you lower your expectations and instead focus on nurturing their natural disposition?
?
Sanusi refutes mukhalafah because it leads to impossible legal conclusions. In your own life, do you sometimes make assumptions that lead to unfair conclusions about others? Are you willing to re-examine your assumptions when they clash with reality, even if it means admitting you were wrong? What practical step can you take today to test one of your assumptions against the real world?

Contradictory Narratives and the True Meaning of Fitrah
Sanusi cites contradictory narrations and concludes fitrah is ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the fitrah is not Islam, but a state of not knowing, as supported by other hadiths and the Quran.

Core Idea
Fitrah is not Islamic belief but the state of not knowing anything.

After rejecting the mukhalafah interpretation, Sanusi presents a narration that a child's religion follows that of his parents, and another hadith that a person's true nature is revealed by their speech after maturity. These demonstrate that the fitrah cannot be Islam itself. Instead, the correct understanding is the Quranic verse 'You know nothing'.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the author presents two new narrations.
2
Identify the parent-following narration
The hadith from Muslim et al. states that a child is considered Jewish, Christian, Zoroastrian, or Muslim based on the parents.
This directly contradicts the idea that every child is born Muslim.
3
Identify the 'tongue expresses' narration
The second hadith indicates that a person's true state is shown after they can speak, either thankful or ungrateful.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that fitrah is not Islam or knowledge of religion, but the state of not knowing, citing Quran 16:78.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً : لا تعارض إلا إذا حملنا الحديث على مفهوم المخالفة، ومفهوم المخالفة لم يتم الاستدلال به؛ لأنه يدل على أن أول ما يولد الإنسان فهو مسلم، وكيف هذا ؟ والطفل دون البلوغ لا يحكم بإسلامه أو كفره إلا إلحاقاً ، فلا تقيم عليه الحد إن ارتد عن الإسلام، ولا نقطع يده إن سرق ما دام دون البلوغ. ولا يتم الاستدلال بمفهوم المخالفة إلا إذا حصرنا المولود بالحالات الأربعة : اليهودية والنصرانية والمجوسية والإسلام والواقع قد يكون غير ذاك كالشيوعية والرأسمالية مثلاً . ولا يصار إلى مفهوم المخالفة ؛ لأنه يوجد رواية لمسلم والبيهقي والحاكم : «فإن كانا مسلمين فمسلم :أي: إن كل مولود إن كان أبواه يهوديين فيهودي، وإن نصرانيين فنصراني، وإن مجوسيين فمجوسي، وإن مُسلمين فمُسلم. وهذا المفهوم خالف نصا نبويًّا آخر وهو : حتى يعرب عنه لسانه، فإن أعرب عنه لسانه فإما شاكراً، وإما كفوراً». فليس يدل ظاهر الحديث ولا منطوقه ولا مفهومه على أن الفطرة هي الإسلام وهي العلم بأمور الدين، بل الصواب أن الفطرة: (لا تعلمون شيئاً) كما دلت عليه الآية .
Secondly: There is no contradiction unless we interpret the hadith according to the concept of opposite meaning (mukhalafah), and the concept of opposite meaning is not used as evidence; because it indicates that when a person is first born he is Muslim, and how is this? The child before puberty is not judged as Muslim or disbeliever except by affiliation, so we do not impose the hadd punishment on him if he apostatizes from Islam, nor do we cut off his hand if he steals as long as he is under puberty. And the concept of opposite meaning is not considered evidence unless we restrict the newborn to the four cases: Judaism, Christianity, Zoroastrianism, and Islam, while reality may be other than that, like communism and capitalism for example. And the concept of opposite meaning is not resorted to because there exists a narration by Muslim, al-Bayhaqi, and al-Hakim: 'If both parents are Muslim, then the child is Muslim' – meaning: every newborn, if his parents are Jews, then he is a Jew; if Christians, then a Christian; if Zoroastrians, then a Zoroastrian; if Muslims, then a Muslim. And this concept contradicts another prophetic text: 'Until his tongue expresses, and if his tongue expresses, then either grateful or ungrateful.' So neither the apparent meaning, nor the expressed meaning, nor the implied meaning of the hadith indicates that fitrah is Islam or knowledge of religious matters. Rather, the correct opinion is that fitrah is: 'You know nothing' as the verse indicates.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a potential objection against the hadith that every child is born in fitrah. It deals with the concept of contrary meaning (mukhalafah) in hadith interpretation. The author argues that one cannot claim the hadith implies that every child is born Muslim by using the principle of mukhalafah, because that principle leads to an impossible legal conclusion: infants cannot be judged as Muslims or disbelievers in a legal sense (e.g., hadd punishments do not apply to minors). Moreover, the principle of mukhalafah only works if the categories are exhaustive, but the mentioned four (Judaism, Christianity, Zoroastrianism, Islam) are not exhaustive in modern times (communism, capitalism). Additionally, there is a direct narration that the child follows the religion of his parents, and another hadith that one's true nature is shown by his speech after maturity. Therefore, the correct interpretation of fitrah is the state of not knowing anything, as stated in the Quran (Surah al-Nahl, 16:78).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the fitrah hadith proves every child is born Muslim with knowledge of God.
Correction: The passage shows that other narrations and the Quran indicate fitrah is a state of ignorance, not pre-existing knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the Quranic verse indicate about fitrah?
A) That fitrah is being born a Muslim.
B) That fitrah is the state of knowing nothing.
C) That fitrah is being born a Jew.
D) That fitrah is the ability to speak.

Key Takeaway
Fitrah is not innate Islam, but a state of not knowing, determined by upbringing and choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely heard that every child is born Muslim. This lesson denies that interpretation. Are you willing to accept that you were born knowing nothing about Allah? If so, how does that make you responsible for your current beliefs? Will you actively seek the truth, or remain content with ignorance?
?
The hadith says your tongue reveals whether you are grateful or ungrateful. When you speak about your Creator, what does your speech show? Have you truly thanked Him for creating you with knowledge, or have you been ungrateful by neglecting to learn?
?
If fitrah is merely a blank slate, then you have no excuse for false beliefs. This lesson strips away the comfort of 'being born Muslim.' Will you now take ownership of your faith, or will you cling to the myth of innate Islam? What step will you take today to replace ignorance with true knowledge?

Self-Knowledge Leads to Knowledge of the Divine
Explanation of the hadith via creation verse and linguistic analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic creation verse illustrates the hadith 'Who knows himself knows his Lord,' and how recognizing our own contingency leads to affirming God's necessary attributes.

Core Idea
Knowing your own limitations reveals the unlimited nature of your Creator.

This passage combines a Quranic verse about human creation with a famous saying often attributed to the Prophet. The author shows that reflecting on our own createdness and dependence directly points to God's eternity and self-sufficiency. It's a powerful example of how self-knowledge becomes a path to theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its explanation
Read the Arabic verse and the author's immediate commentary. Notice how 'man' is first Adam, then his descendants—a rhetorical device called istikhdam.
This device uses one word in two senses in the same context.
2
Understand the hadith and its meaning
Read the hadith 'Whoever knows himself knows his Lord' and the author's gloss: knowing oneself as originated and needy leads to knowing God as eternal and self-sufficient.
3
Connect the two
See how the verse's portrayal of human creation (from clay, then as a drop) illustrates the hadith's meaning of origination and need.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider how this establishes a core kalam argument: contingency of the creation implies a necessary, self-sufficient Creator.
This is a foundational argument for God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال تعالى: ﴿وَلَقَدْ خَلَقْنَا الْإِنسَانَ مِن سُلَالَةٍ مِّن طِينٍ ثُمَّ جَعَلْتَهُ نُطْفَةً فِي قَرارِ مكين [المؤمنون: ۱۲-۱۳] الآية والإنسان: آدم؛ والسلالة الطينة، قطعة : فهي من عموم الطين والضمير في قوله ثمَّ جَعَلْتَهُ نُطْفَةً ﴾ [المؤمنون: ١٣] عائد للإنسان لا يمعنى ،آدم بل بمعنى بنيه، ففيه استخدام (۱) ، وقد ورد من عرف نفسَهُ عرف ربَّهُ) (٢) أي : من عرف نفسه بالحدوث والفقر، عرف ربَّه بالقدم والغنى، وهذا هو الأظهر في معنى
And He said, exalted: 'And certainly We created man from an extract of clay. Then We made him a drop in a secure resting place' (Quran 23:12-13) – the verse. And man here is Adam; and 'extract' (sulala) is the clay, a piece: so it is from the generality of clay. And the pronoun in 'then We made him a drop' refers to man, not meaning Adam but meaning his descendants, so there is usage (istikhdam). And it has been reported: 'Whoever knows himself knows his Lord' – i.e., whoever knows himself as originated and needy knows his Lord as eternal and self-sufficient, and this is the most apparent meaning.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage begins by citing the Quranic verse about the creation of man from clay and then as a drop. The comment explains that 'man' here refers specifically to Adam, and the 'extract' is the clay. Then the pronoun in 'then We made him a drop' refers not to Adam but to his progeny, employing a rhetorical device called istikhdam (using a word in two different senses in one context). Then the well-known saying (often attributed as a hadith) is quoted: 'Whoever knows himself knows his Lord.' The author explains its meaning: knowing oneself as originated and in need leads to knowing one's Lord as eternal (qadim) and self-sufficient (ghani). This interpretation is considered the most apparent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-knowledge equals divine knowledge or leads to claiming divinity.
Correction: The hadith means recognizing one's own contingency (origination and need) leads to affirming God's necessary attributes (eternity and self-sufficiency), not that one becomes divine.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the meaning of 'Whoever knows himself knows his Lord'?
A) He knows his Lord directly
B) He knows his own origination and need, hence knows God's eternity and self-sufficiency
C) He becomes God
D) He knows his own soul

Key Takeaway
Self-knowledge of our own createdness and dependence leads directly to knowledge of God's eternity and self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you honestly examine your own existence—your beginning from a drop of fluid and your complete dependence on forces beyond your control—does your heart genuinely acknowledge that you are needy and originated? Or do you live as if you are self-sufficient? What would change today if you truly lived with the reality of your faqr?
?
The hadith says 'whoever knows himself knows his Lord.' But have you ever stopped to consider that your very inability to know your own soul fully (your deepest motives, your future, the moment of your death) actually points to a Lord who knows everything? How does that thought humble your pride right now?
?
If knowing yourself leads to knowing God, what is the one thing about yourself that you have been avoiding acknowledging—your mortality, your weakness, your sin—that if admitted, would bring you closer to your Creator? Are you willing to face it today?

Angels Required for Detailed Knowledge
Listing angels requiring detailed knowledge with ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which angels must be known in detail (tafsil) and why denial of them constitutes disbelief, as well as the exception for Munkar and Nakeer.

Core Idea
Detailed knowledge of specific angels is obligatory; denial of those is disbelief, but Munkar and Nakeer are an exception.

This lesson identifies the angels that every Muslim must know in detail and the ruling on denying them. You'll see that some angels are listed individually, while others like Munkar and Nakeer are not among them due to scholarly disagreement. The author also clarifies that belief in the bearers of the Throne is required only in summary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of angels for detailed knowledge
Read the passage and list the angels mentioned: Jibril, Mika'il, Israfil, Ridwan, Malik, Raqeeb, and Ateed. Note that the text also references Azraeel as the Angel of Death, but the name is not definitive.
These angels are known from definitive Quranic and hadith evidence.
2
Understand the ruling on denial
The passage states that denying any of these angels constitutes disbelief (kufr). This is because their existence is established by certain evidence.
3
Recognize the exception: Munkar and Nakeer
Unlike the detailed list, denying Munkar and Nakeer does not lead to disbelief because there is scholarly disagreement about the reality of the questioning in the grave.
This exception shows that matters of dispute do not necessitate takfir.
4
Learn about summary belief
The passage also obligates belief in the bearers of the Throne and those surrounding it, but only in summary (ijmal), similar to other angels not specified in detail.
Summary belief means affirming their existence without needing to know their names or specifics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعزرائيل (۲)
،
ما يجبُ معرفتُهُ مِنَ الملائكة]

Azraeel is mentioned with a note that it refers to the Angel of Death, but the name is not definitive. The ruling is that denial of any of these angels constitutes disbelief. However, Munkar and Nakeer are an exception: denial of them does not entail disbelief because there is disagreement among scholars about the reality of the questioning in the grave. The passage also states that belief in the bearers of the Throne and those around it is only required in summary (ijmal), like the rest of the angels.

Required Knowledge of Angels: Detailed and General
Lists angels requiring detailed vs general belief, with notes on Munkar and Nakir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which angels require detailed knowing versus general belief, and why denying Munkar and Nakir does not constitute disbelief.

Core Idea
Faith in angels requires knowing some by name but only believing generally in others.

Angels are a fundamental article of faith, but Islamic theology distinguishes between angels that must be known individually and those that require only general belief. This passage from the Jawharat al-Tawhid explains that distinction and addresses a common question about Munkar and Nakir.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Start by reading the passage carefully to get an overview. Note the structure: it first lists angels that must be known in detail, then discusses Munkar and Nakir as an exception, and finally states the general belief required for other angels.
Pay attention to the footnote numbers in the text.
2
Identify the two categories of angelic knowledge
The passage clearly divides knowledge of angels into two types: detailed (tafsil) and general (ijmal). List the angels that belong to each category.
Detailed: Jibril, Mika'il, Israfil, Ridwan, Malik, Raqib, 'Atid. General: bearers of the Throne and those surrounding it.
3
Understand the exception of Munkar and Nakir
The text states that denying Munkar and Nakir does not constitute kufr because there is disagreement about the reality of the grave questioning. Think about why this nuance matters for defining the limits of disbelief.
This exception highlights that not every denial of a non-definitive belief reaches the level of kufr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يجبُ معرفتُهُ مِنَ الملائكة] والجمعُ الذي يجب معرفتُهُ تفصيلاً من الملائكة جبريل وميكائيل، وإسرافيل، ورضوان خازن الجنة، ومالك خازن النار، ورقيب وعتيد، فيكفر منكر شيء من ذلك، وأما مُنْكَرُ ونَكِيرٌ فلا يكفرُ منكرُهما ؛ لأنه اختلف في أصل السؤال. ويجب الإيمان بحملة العرش والحافين به إجمالاً كسائر الملائكة . (1) فمن غفَلَ أو لم يداوم حضور أسماء الواجب الإيمان بهم لا يضرُّهُ. (2) والمراد ملك الموت دون تعين اسمه المذكور هنا، إذ لم يرد إلا في آثار عن وهب بن منبه كما في العظمة» لأبي الشيخ (٣٩٤، ٤٣٩).
What must be known of the angels: and the group whose knowledge is required in detail from among the angels are Jibril, Mika'il, Israfil, Ridwan the keeper of Paradise, Malik the keeper of Hell, Raqib and 'Atid; so the denier of any of these commits kufr. As for Munkar and Nakir, their denier does not commit kufr because there is disagreement on the original [question of the] questioning. And it is obligatory to believe in the bearers of the Throne and those surrounding it in general like the rest of the angels. (1) Whoever is heedless or does not continuously attend to the names of those whose belief is obligatory is not harmed by [his ignorance]. (2) What is meant is the Angel of Death, not the specific name mentioned here, since it is only reported in traditions from Wahb ibn Munabbih as in al-'Azamah by Abu al-Shaykh (394, 439).
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage classifies the knowledge of angels into detailed and general categories. It lists angels that must be known in detail: Jibril, Mika'il, Israfil, Ridwan (keeper of Paradise), Malik (keeper of Hell), Raqib, and 'Atid. Denying any of these is kufr. However, Munkar and Nakir are exceptions: denying them is not kufr because there is scholarly disagreement about the reality of the questioning in the grave (the original source of their names). The passage then notes that belief in the bearers of the Throne and those surrounding it is required in general, like the rest of the angels. The footnotes clarify two points: (1) a person who is heedless of the names of the angels whose belief is obligatory is not harmed by that ignorance; (2) the angel of death (Malak al-Mawt) is meant, but his specific name is not definitively established in authentic texts, only in Isra'iliyyat.

Correction: Denying Munkar and Nakir is not disbelief because the evidence for the grave questioning is not definitive; thus, the rule of kufr does not apply.

Grammatical Analysis of Quran 3:7 on al-Rasikhun fi al-‘Ilm
Commentator analyzes two grammatical stops in Quran 3:7.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two possible grammatical stops in Quran 3:7 affect the interpretation of who knows the ta’wil (interpretation) of ambiguous verses.

Core Idea
A grammatical pause can change theological meaning.

This lesson explores a classic debate in Quranic exegesis: does the verse “None knows its interpretation except Allah” also include “those firmly rooted in knowledge”? The answer depends on where you pause when reciting. We will examine both grammatical possibilities and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage from the commentary. Notice the two grammatical analyses presented. The passage discusses two possible places to stop (waqf) in the verse.
2
Understand the two stops
The first analysis treats 'al-rasikhun' as conjoined to 'Allah', meaning they also know the ta'wil. The second analysis stops after 'Allah', so the rasikhun are separate and simply say 'We believe in it'.
These are the two main interpretations among scholars.
3
See the theological consequence
The comment then mentions the Mujassimah (anthropomorphists) as an example of those with deviation. Understanding the grammatical options helps refute false interpretations.
This shows the practical importance of seemingly technical grammar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بناءً على أن الوقف على قوله تعالى : والرَّاسِخُونَ فِي الْعِلْمِ ﴾ [آل عمران: ٧] فيكون معطوفاً على لفظ الجلالة، وعلى هذا فنظم الآية هكذا: (وما يعلم تأويله إلا الله والراسخون في العلم)، وجملة يَقُولُونَ ءَامَنَّا به [آل عمران: ٧] حينئذٍ مستأنفة لبيان سبب التماس التأويل، أو على قوله: ﴿وَمَا يَعْلَمُ تَأْوِيلَهُ إِلَّا اللهُ ﴾ [آل عمران : ٧] وعلى هذا فقوله: ﴿وَالرَّسَخُونَ فِي الْعِلْمِ) [آل عمران : ٧] ، إلى آخره استئناف، وذكر مقابله في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَاما الَّذِينَ فِي قُلُوبِهِمْ زَيْعُ ) [آل عمران : ١٧ إلى آخره؛ أي: كالمجسمة فمنهم من قال : إنه على صورة شيخ كبير، ومنهم من قال : إنه على صورة شاب حسن، تعالى الله عن ذلك علوا كبيراً .
Based on the stop (waqf) being on His saying, Most High: {And those firmly rooted in knowledge} (Al Imran 7), so it would be conjoined to the word of majesty (Allah). According to this, the order of the verse is: 'And none knows its interpretation except Allah and those firmly rooted in knowledge.' And the sentence {they say: 'We believe in it'} (Al Imran 7) is then a new beginning explaining the reason for seeking interpretation. Or [the stop is] on His saying: {And none knows its interpretation except Allah} (Al Imran 7). According to this, His saying: {And those firmly rooted in knowledge} (Al Imran 7) to the end is a new beginning, and its counterpart is mentioned in His saying: {As for those in whose hearts is deviation} (Al Imran 7) to the end; i.e., like the Mujassimah (anthropomorphists), among them some say: He is in the form of an old man, and some say: He is in the form of a handsome young man. Exalted is Allah above that, with great exaltation.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage examines the grammatical structure of Quran 3:7 regarding the 'rasikhun fi al-'ilm' (those firmly rooted in knowledge). The commentator presents two possible interpretations based on where one pauses (waqf). If the pause is after 'Allah' and 'al-rasikhun' is treated as a conjoined noun (ma'tuf), then the meaning is that Allah and the rasikhun know the ta'wil (interpretation of ambiguous verses). The phrase 'they say: We believe in it' then begins a new sentence explaining why they seek interpretation. Alternatively, if the pause is after 'Allah' such that the rasikhun are not part of the knowing, then the verse continues as a new sentence: 'But those firmly rooted in knowledge say...' The commentator then notes that the counterpart of this group (those with deviation) is illustrated by the Mujassimah (anthropomorphists) who attribute to Allah a form such as an old man or a young man, which is far exalted above Allah. This passage shows how grammatical choices affect theological meaning.

Key Terms
الوقف
الراسخون في العلم
التأويل
المجسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse definitively proves that only Allah knows the ta'wil of ambiguous verses.
Correction: Grammatical analysis shows that the verse can be read with 'al-rasikhun' conjoined to 'Allah', meaning they also know the ta'wil. Many scholars hold this view.

Checkpoint

A) Only Allah
B) Only the rasikhun
C) Both Allah and the rasikhun
D) No one

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of Quran 3:7 depends on a grammatical pause, showing how Arabic syntax directly impacts Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Quranic verses that are ambiguous, do you humbly say 'We believe in it' like the rasikhun, or do you feel compelled to force an interpretation without proper knowledge? Which posture reflects your heart right now?
?

?
The Mujassimah are cited as an example of deviation – people who took literal meanings to extremes. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah that are not firmly grounded in the Quran and Sunnah as understood by the scholars? Will you examine them today?

God's Promise Never Fails
Explains that God necessarily fulfills His promise to believers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's fulfillment of His promise is necessary and that Ash'aris and Maturidis agree on this.

Core Idea
Breaking a promise is a deficiency; God is free from all deficiency.

In this lesson, we explore the first part of the commentator's explanation of the poem. He clarifies that God's promise of Paradise to believers is absolutely fulfilled, as supported by Qur'anic verses and rational argument. You'll learn why this is a point of agreement between Ash'aris and Maturidis.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the first part of the passage up to the agreement of the two schools. Let the text speak for itself before analysis.
Notice the logical structure: definition, evidence, argument, consensus.
2
Identify the Qur'anic evidence
Two verses are cited: al-Rum 30:6 and Al 'Imran 3:9. Understand how they support the claim that God never breaks His promise.
The commentator interprets 'al-mi'ād' as 'al-wa'd' in this context.
3
Follow the rational argument
The argument is a conditional: if God broke His promise, then lying and foolishness would follow. Since these are impossible for God, breaking a promise is impossible.
This is a basic qiyās al-‘aks (argument by contradiction).
4
Note the scholarly consensus
Both Ash'aris and Maturidis agree that breaking a promise is a deficiency from which God must be exonerated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَمُنْجِزُ لِمَنْ أَرَادَ وَعْدَهُ) أي : ومُعْط للذي أراد به خيراً ما وعده به على لسان نبيه أو في كتابه فمفعول (( أراد) محذوف، و(وعده) مفعولُ (منجز)، والمراد به الموعود به، وأشار المصنف بذلك إلى أن وعد الله المؤمنين الجنة لا يتخلف شرعاً قطعاً؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَعْدَ اللَّهِ لَا يُخْلِفُ اللهُ وَعْدَهُ [الروم: ٦، وإن اللهَ لَا يُخْلِفُ الْمِيعَادَ﴾ [آل عمران: ٩]؛ أي: الوعد كما قاله بعض المفسرين، فلو تخلَّفَ إعطاء الموعود به لزم الكذب والسفه والخُلْفُ، واللازم باطل فكذا الملزوم، فالخُلْفُ في الوعد نقص يجب تنزيه الله عنه، وهذا متفق عليه عند الأشاعرة والماتريدية. وأمَّا الوعيد فيجوز الخُلْف فيه عند الأشاعرة؛ لأن الخُلْفَ فيه لا يُعدُّ نقصاً، يُعد كرما يُمتدح به (٢) ، كما يشير له قول الشاعر: [من الطويل] بَلْ سُقْمِيَ فِي الْحُبِّ عَافِيَتِي وَوُجُودِيَ مِنَ الْهَوَى عَدَمِي
His statement: 'And the fulfiller (munjiz) for whom He wills His promise' means: the Giver to the one with whom He wills good of what He promised him upon the tongue of His Prophet or in His Book. The object of 'arāda' is omitted, and 'waʿdahu' is the object of 'munjiz', and what is intended by it is the promised thing. The author indicated by this that Allah's promise of Paradise to the believers does not fail to be fulfilled according to divine law absolutely, because of His statement, 'The promise of Allah: Allah does not break His promise' [al-Rum: 6], and 'Indeed Allah does not break the appointment' [Al 'Imran: 9], i.e., the promise, as some commentators said. For if the giving of the promised thing were to fail, lying, foolishness, and breaking the promise would follow, and the consequent is false, so likewise the antecedent. So breaking the promise is a deficiency from which Allah must be exonerated. This is agreed upon by the Ash'aris and Maturidis. As for the threat (waʿīd), breaking it is permissible according to the Ash'aris, because breaking it is not considered a deficiency; rather it is considered generosity (karam) for which one is praised, as the poet indicates: [ṭawīl] 'My illness in love is my recovery, and my existence in passion is my non-existence.'
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage explains the line 'Wa munjiz liman arāda waʿdahu' from the poem. The commentator begins by clarifying the meaning: God gives what He promised to those with whom He wills good. The phrase is a reference to God's fulfillment of His promise of Paradise for believers. The commentator asserts that God's promise never fails, as supported by two Qur'anic verses: al-Rum 30:6 and Al 'Imran 3:9. The reasoning is that if God failed to give the promised reward, it would imply lying, foolishness, and breaking a promise—all of which are deficient and must be negated of God. This point is agreed upon by both Ash'aris and Maturidis. However, regarding threats (waʿīd), such as punishment for sin, the Ash'aris hold that God may legitimately withhold punishment. This is not considered a deficiency but an act of generosity (karam) praiseworthy in itself. The commentator cites a line of poetry to illustrate this concept, although the full verse is not given.

Key Terms
وعد
خلف الوعد
منجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God might fail to fulfill His promise if He wishes.
Correction: God's promise is absolutely binding because breaking it entails deficiency, and God is free from deficiency.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does God not break His promise?
A) Because it would be impossible for Him.
B) Because breaking a promise is a deficiency and God is free from deficiency.
C) Because He only threatens but does not promise.
D) Because He is compelled by human actions.

Key Takeaway
God's fulfillment of His promise is necessary to avoid deficiency; this is agreed upon by Ash'aris and Maturidis.

God's Threat: An Act of Generosity
Explains that breaking a threat is permissible for God as generosity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'aris hold that God may break His threat (wa'id) as an act of generosity, unlike His promise.

Core Idea
Breaking a threat is not a deficiency; it is praiseworthy generosity.

Having established that God's promise must be fulfilled, the commentator now turns to the topic of threats (wa'id). He explains that according to Ash'aris, God may withhold punishment from sinners, and this is not a flaw but a virtue. You'll learn the reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Focus on the lines discussing wa'id. Notice the contrast with the previous section on wa'd.
The passage explicitly states the difference: breaking a threat is generosity, not deficiency.
2
Understand the key difference
Breaking a promise is a deficiency, but breaking a threat is considered an act of generosity (karam). This is the core distinction.
3
Reflect on the poetic illustration
The poet's line illustrates how something seemingly negative (illness, non-existence) can be positive in context, supporting the idea that breaking a threat can be praiseworthy.
The verse is not fully quoted but gives a sense of paradoxical generosity.
4
Note the scope of the ruling
The passage says this is the position of the Ash'aris. It does not mention Maturidis here, so the ruling is specific to Ash'ari doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَمُنْجِزُ لِمَنْ أَرَادَ وَعْدَهُ) أي : ومُعْط للذي أراد به خيراً ما وعده به على لسان نبيه أو في كتابه فمفعول (( أراد) محذوف، و(وعده) مفعولُ (منجز)، والمراد به الموعود به، وأشار المصنف بذلك إلى أن وعد الله المؤمنين الجنة لا يتخلف شرعاً قطعاً؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَعْدَ اللَّهِ لَا يُخْلِفُ اللهُ وَعْدَهُ [الروم: ٦، وإن اللهَ لَا يُخْلِفُ الْمِيعَادَ﴾ [آل عمران: ٩]؛ أي: الوعد كما قاله بعض المفسرين، فلو تخلَّفَ إعطاء الموعود به لزم الكذب والسفه والخُلْفُ، واللازم باطل فكذا الملزوم، فالخُلْفُ في الوعد نقص يجب تنزيه الله عنه، وهذا متفق عليه عند الأشاعرة والماتريدية. وأمَّا الوعيد فيجوز الخُلْف فيه عند الأشاعرة؛ لأن الخُلْفَ فيه لا يُعدُّ نقصاً، يُعد كرما يُمتدح به (٢) ، كما يشير له قول الشاعر: [من الطويل] بَلْ سُقْمِيَ فِي الْحُبِّ عَافِيَتِي وَوُجُودِيَ مِنَ الْهَوَى عَدَمِي
His statement: 'And the fulfiller (munjiz) for whom He wills His promise' means: the Giver to the one with whom He wills good of what He promised him upon the tongue of His Prophet or in His Book. The object of 'arāda' is omitted, and 'waʿdahu' is the object of 'munjiz', and what is intended by it is the promised thing. The author indicated by this that Allah's promise of Paradise to the believers does not fail to be fulfilled according to divine law absolutely, because of His statement, 'The promise of Allah: Allah does not break His promise' [al-Rum: 6], and 'Indeed Allah does not break the appointment' [Al 'Imran: 9], i.e., the promise, as some commentators said. For if the giving of the promised thing were to fail, lying, foolishness, and breaking the promise would follow, and the consequent is false, so likewise the antecedent. So breaking the promise is a deficiency from which Allah must be exonerated. This is agreed upon by the Ash'aris and Maturidis. As for the threat (waʿīd), breaking it is permissible according to the Ash'aris, because breaking it is not considered a deficiency; rather it is considered generosity (karam) for which one is praised, as the poet indicates: [ṭawīl] 'My illness in love is my recovery, and my existence in passion is my non-existence.'
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage explains the line 'Wa munjiz liman arāda waʿdahu' from the poem. The commentator begins by clarifying the meaning: God gives what He promised to those with whom He wills good. The phrase is a reference to God's fulfillment of His promise of Paradise for believers. The commentator asserts that God's promise never fails, as supported by two Qur'anic verses: al-Rum 30:6 and Al 'Imran 3:9. Correction: According to Ash'aris, threats can be broken as an act of generosity, while promises must be fulfilled to avoid deficiency.

The Sharia Ruling on Breaking Promises vs Threats
The author distinguishes between rational and Sharia perspectives on reward and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sharia distinction between breaking a promise and breaking a threat, and why one is impossible for God while the other is permissible.

Core Idea
God always keeps promises, but may graciously forgo threats.

This lesson explores a key distinction in divine justice and mercy. While reason allows God to act as He wills, revelation sets boundaries: a promise is a commitment that must be honored, but a threat can be withdrawn as an act of grace. The author clarifies why breaking a promise would be foolishness, impossible for God, while breaking a threat is generosity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author references an earlier discussion for verification.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two distinct rulings: one for breaking a promise (khalf al-wa'd) and one for breaking a threat (khalf al-wa'id). Note the key terms and the reason given for each.
The reasons are opposite: foolishness vs generosity.
3
Connect to rational framework
The author says 'as the verification of that has preceded.' Recall that reason alone allows both reward and punishment without restriction. The Sharia adds specific constraints based on God's attributes.
This shows how revelation complements reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بحسب الشرع فلا يجوز خلف الوعد؛ لأنَّه سَفَهُ وهو يستحيل عليه تعالى، وأما الوعيد فيجوز الخلفُ فيه؛ لأنَّه كرمٌ وفضل، كما تقدَّم تحقيق ذلك.
As for according to the Sharia, breaking a promise is not permissible because it is foolishness, and that is impossible for Him, exalted. As for breaking a threat, it is permissible because it is generosity and favor, as the verification of that has preceded.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage provides the Sharia (revealed law) perspective on the issue of reward and punishment. While the rational argument allowed God to punish the obedient and reward the disobedient, the Sharia imposes restrictions: God cannot break a promise (khalf al-wa'd) because that would be foolishness (safah), which is impossible for Him. However, breaking a threat (khalf al-wa'id) is permissible because it is an act of generosity (karm) and favor (fadl). The distinction is based on the nature of promises and threats: a promise is a commitment that must be honored, whereas a threat can be waived as an act of grace. The author references earlier discussion for fuller verification.

Key Terms
خلف الوعد
خلف الوعيد
السفه
الكرم
الفضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God can do anything, then breaking a threat is a sign of inconsistency or injustice.
Correction: Breaking a threat is not an imperfection; it is an act of grace. The passage clarifies that God's threats can be forgone out of generosity, which is consistent with His mercy.

Checkpoint

A) Because it would contradict His justice.
B) Because it would be foolishness (safah).
C) Because it would diminish His power.
D) Because He never made any promises.

Key Takeaway
In Sharia, God's promise must be fulfilled, but His threat may be mercifully lifted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What specific promise are you most struggling to rely on, and what step can you take this week to act on that trust?

?

Now, how does that change the way you treat others when they wrong you? Will you choose to be generous like your Lord, or will you insist on your threats?

Hadith Evidence for Belief in Qada and Qadr
Cites hadiths proving that belief in Qada and Qadr is obligatory and explains the use of scriptural proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that belief in qada and qadr is established by scriptural evidence (dalil sam'i) and why the scholars preferred this approach for the general public.

Core Idea
Faith in qadr comes from scripture, not reason alone.

In this passage, the author explains the famous phrase 'as has come in the report' and clarifies that the proof for belief in qada and qadr is scriptural. He then cites two key hadiths that make belief in qadr a condition of faith. Finally, he explains why the scholars chose to rely on these transmitted proofs rather than purely rational arguments—a practical decision that makes faith accessible to all.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'al-khabar' and its synonymity
Read the opening where the author explains 'as has come in the report' and learns that 'khabar' is synonymous with 'hadith'. Note the verse from al-Sabban's poem.
2
Read the hadith from 'Ali (ra)
Study the first hadith narrated from 'Ali, which lists four essentials of faith including belief in qadr. Understand that this hadith makes qadr a condition of complete faith.
The hadith uses the phrase 'its good and its evil, its sweet and its bitter'.
3
Read the hadith of the forty
Examine the second hadith, from the well-known hadith of Jibril, which also includes belief in qadr as part of iman.
This is the famous hadith of the forty often studied in hadith collections.
4
Reflect on the reason for scriptural proof
Consider the author's statement that scholars relied on scriptural proof here because it is easier for the common people. Connect this to the earlier discussion that qadr is rationally knowable but scripture provides a simpler path.
Even though qadr relates to attributes which have rational proofs, the scriptural approach is pedagogically superior.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (كَمَا أَتَى فِي الخَبَرِ ) أي : لما ورد في الخبر، فالكاف للتعليل، والمراد من الخبر الحديث؛ لأنَّ الخبر والحديث مترادفان على الأصح، ولذلك قال العلامة الصبَّانُ في منظومته التي في المصطلح : [من الرجز]
والخَبَرُ المَتْنُ الحَدِيثُ الأَثَرُ مَا عَنْ إمَامِ المُرْسَلينَ يُؤْتَرُ
أَو غَيْرُهُ لَا فَرْقَ فِيْمَا اعْتَمَدَا
وأشار المصنِّفُ بذلك إلى أن دليل ذلك سمعيٌّ ؛ فمن جملة ذلك ما رُوي عن عليّ كرَّم الله وجهه أنه قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : لا يؤمنُ عبد حتّى يؤمن بأربعة : يشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وأنّي رسولُ اللهِ بعثني بالحقِّ، ويؤمن بالبعث بعد الموت، ويؤمن بالقدر خيره وشره، حلوه ومُرِّهِ ، ومن جملة ذلك أيضاً حديث الأربعين: «الإيمان أن تؤمن بالله وملائكته وكتبه ورسله، وتؤمنَ بالقَدَرِ خيره وشره، حلوه ومره، وإِنَّما عوّلوا على الدليل السمعي هنا ؛ لأنه أسهل للعامة، وإلا فقد علمت مما مرَّ أنَّ القضاء والقَدَر يرجعان للصفات التي عوّلوا فيها على الدليل العقلي.
His statement: 'as has come in the report (al-khabar)' means: as has occurred in the report, for the kaf (of ka-ma) is for causation, and what is meant by 'report' is hadith, because 'report' and 'hadith' are synonymous according to the correct view. That is why the scholar al-Sabban said in his didactic poem on terminology (in rajaz meter): / 'Al-khabar, al-matn, al-hadith, al-athar are what is transmitted from the leader of the messengers / Or from other than him, there is no difference in what they relied upon.' / And by this the author indicated that its proof is scriptural (sam'i). Among that is what was narrated from 'Ali, may God honor his face, that he said: The Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, said: 'A servant does not believe until he believes in four: he bears witness that there is no god but God and that I am the Messenger of God sent with the truth, and he believes in the resurrection after death, and he believes in al-qadr, its good and its evil, its sweet and its bitter.' And among that also is the hadith of the forty: 'Faith is that you believe in God, His angels, His books, His messengers, and you believe in al-qadr, its good and its evil, its sweet and its bitter.' And they only relied on scriptural proof here because it is easier for the common people; otherwise, you have known from what has passed that al-qada and al-qadr refer back to the attributes for which they relied on rational proof.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage explains the author's phrase 'as has come in the report.' It clarifies that 'al-khabar' (report) refers to hadith, and cites a line from al-Sabban's poem stating that khabar, matn, hadith, and athar are synonymous when transmitted from the Prophet. The author then states that the proof for belief in qada and qadr is scriptural (sam'i), exemplified by two hadiths: the hadith of 'Ali listing four essentials of faith including belief in qadr, and the hadith of the forty (often the hadith of Jibril) defining iman as including belief in qadr. The author explains that the scholars relied on scriptural evidence here because it is easier for the general public, even though qada and qadr rationally reduce to attributes whose proofs are rational. This highlights the pedagogical approach of using accessible proofs for essential beliefs.

الخبر (al-khabar)
الحديث (hadith)
الإيمان (iman)
القدر (al-qadr)
الدليل السمعي (dalil sam'i)
الدليل العقلي (dalil aqli)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that qada and qadr are only known by reason, and scripture is secondary.
Correction: The passage shows that scriptural evidence is primary for faith; scholars presented these hadiths as the foundation of belief in qadr, especially for the general public.

Checkpoint

A) rational proof is impossible

C) the hadith are stronger than rational arguments
D) the attributes are not knowable by reason

Key Takeaway
Belief in qadr is a required part of iman, established by prophetic hadith, and scholars present this proof because it is easier for the common people.

The Scriptural Proof for Qada and Qadar
Commentary explaining the scriptural basis for qada and qadar and the method behind it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why scholars cited scriptural proof for qada and qadar in this context and how it relates to rational proofs for the divine attributes.

Core Idea
Qada and qadar are affirmed by both revelation and reason, but scripture is easier for the common person.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the author's allusion to prophetic hadiths as evidence for qada and qadar. He clarifies the terminology of 'khabar' and 'hadith', then presents two key hadiths that link faith to belief in divine decree. He also explains the pedagogical reason for relying on transmitted proof here: it is more accessible to the general public, even though the underlying attributes are rationally established.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic text from the source chunk. Notice the commentary structure and the two hadiths cited.
2
Understand the terminology
The commentator equates 'khabar' and 'hadith' as synonyms. Absorb the quote from al-Sabban's poem on terminology.
3
Identify the two hadiths
Find the hadith narrated by Ali and the 'hadith of forty'. Note how each explicitly includes belief in qadar as a condition of faith.
4
Grasp the methodological point
Understand why scriptural proof is used here: it is easier for the common people, even though qada and qadar ultimately reduce to attributes proven by reason.
This shows the complementary roles of sam‘ and ‘aql.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (كَمَا أَتَى فِي الخَبَرِ ) أي : لما ورد في الخبر، فالكاف للتعليل، والمراد من الخبر الحديث؛ لأنَّ الخبر والحديث مترادفان على الأصح، ولذلك قال العلامة الصبَّانُ في منظومته التي في المصطلح : [من الرجز] والخَبَرُ المَتْنُ الحَدِيثُ الأَثَرُ مَا عَنْ إمَامِ المُرْسَلينَ يُؤْتَرُ أَو غَيْرُهُ لَا فَرْقَ فِيْمَا اعْتَمَدَا وأشار المصنِّفُ بذلك إلى أن دليل ذلك سمعيٌّ ؛ فمن جملة ذلك ما رُوي عن عليّ كرَّم الله وجهه أنه قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : لا يؤمنُ عبد حتّى يؤمن بأربعة : يشهد أن لا إله إلا الله وأنّي رسولُ اللهِ بعثني بالحقِّ، ويؤمن بالبعث بعد الموت، ويؤمن بالقدر خيره وشره، حلوه ومُرِّهِ (۱) ، ومن جملة ذلك أيضاً حديث الأربعين: «الإيمان أن تؤمن بالله وملائكته وكتبه ورسله، وتؤمنَ بالقَدَرِ خيره وشره، حلوه ومره (۲) ، وإِنَّما عوّلوا على الدليل السمعي هنا ؛ لأنه أسهل للعامة، وإلا فقد علمت مما مرَّ أنَّ القضاء والقَدَر يرجعان للصفات التي عوّلوا فيها على الدليل العقلي.
His saying: (as came in the khabar) means: because it came in the khabar, so the kaf is for causation, and what is meant by khabar is hadith; because khabar and hadith are synonymous according to the more correct view. That is why al-Allama al-Sabban said in his poem on terminology: [from rajaz] 'Khabar, matn, hadith, athar, whatever is transmitted from the Messenger of messengers or from others, no difference in what they rely on.' The author indicated by this that the proof for that is scriptural. Among that is what was narrated from Ali, may Allah honor his face, that he said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'No servant believes until he believes in four things: that he bears witness that there is no god but Allah and that I am the Messenger of Allah sent with the truth, and he believes in the resurrection after death, and he believes in qadar, its good and its evil, its sweet and its bitter.' And among that is also the hadith of forty: 'Faith is that you believe in Allah, His angels, His books, His messengers, and you believe in qadar, its good and its evil, its sweet and its bitter.' They relied on scriptural proof here because it is easier for the common people; otherwise, you have already learned from what has passed that qada and qadar go back to the attributes which they relied on rational proof.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the line 'kama ata fi al-khabar' in the poem. The commentator first clarifies the meaning of 'khabar' and 'hadith', stating they are synonymous. He quotes al-Sabban's poetic definition. Then he explains that the author pointed out that the proof for qada and qadar in this context is scriptural (sam'i). He supports this by citing two hadiths: one from Ali on the four essential beliefs including qadar, and the well-known hadith of the forty. The commentator then gives the reason for relying on scriptural evidence here: it is easier for the common people. However, he reminds the reader that qada and qadar ultimately reduce to the divine attributes (knowledge, will, power) which are proven by rational proof. This lesson shows the complementary roles of transmitted and rational proofs in kalam.

Key Terms
الخبر
الحديث
الدليل السمعي
القضاء والقدر
الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think qada and qadar are only known by revelation and have no rational basis.
Correction: The passage shows that while scriptural proof is given for accessibility, qada and qadar reduce to the divine attributes (knowledge, will, power) which are proven rationally.

Checkpoint
Why did the scholars rely on scriptural proof for qada and qadar in this context rather than rational proof?
A) Because rational proof is invalid for qada and qadar
B) Because it is easier for the common people
C) Because the hadiths are more authentic
D) Because qada and qadar are not rational matters

Key Takeaway
The scriptural proof for qada and qadar is presented for ease of understanding, but the doctrine is ultimately rooted in rational attributes of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face unexpected hardship, do you truly embrace that qadar includes 'its evil and its bitter', or do you only credit Allah with the good while attributing the bad to chance or others? What does your reaction reveal about your real belief?
?
This lesson shows that scriptural proof is given for the common person. Are you content to merely accept qadar on authority, or do you actively seek to understand the rational attributes of Allah that underpin it? What step will you take this week to deepen that understanding?
?
The hadith says a servant does not truly believe until he believes in qadar. Examine your heart: is there any part of your life where you resist submitting to Allah's decree, secretly wishing things were different? Will you surrender that area to Him today?

The Hadith Proof for Seeing Allah
Hadith proof for ru'ya and refutation of Mu'tazila interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith about seeing Allah like the full moon serves as a proof for ru'ya and how the Mu'tazila misinterpreted it.

Core Idea
The hadith compares clarity of vision, not Allah Himself, to the moon.

The Sunnah provides clear evidence for the believers' vision of Allah in the afterlife. The famous hadith comparing seeing Allah to the full moon is often misunderstood. This lesson examines the hadith, clarifies its meaning, and addresses the Mu'tazila reinterpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith citation
Start by reading the Arabic text of the hadith about seeing the Lord like the moon. Then read the translation.
Notice the word 'sa' in 'satarawna'.
2
Understand the clarification
The commentator clarifies that the comparison (tashbih) is about the act of seeing, not the seen object. Understand why this distinction is important.
This guards against anthropomorphism.
3
Learn the Mu'tazila interpretation
The Mu'tazila reinterpreted the hadith to mean seeing Allah's mercy, not Allah Himself. Reflect on why this is rejected.
The passage does not give a detailed refutation, but implies it is wrong.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السنَّةُ فأحاديث كحديث : إنَّكم سترون ربّكم كما ترون القمر ليلة البدره (۳) ، والتشبيه للرؤية في عدم الشكّ والخفاء، لا للمرئي كما قد يتوهم، والتعبير بالسين في الحديث؛ لأنَّ القيامة قد قَرُبت وأَوَّلَ المعتزلة الحديث بأنَّ المعنى : سترون رحمة ربكم. وأما الإجماع فهو أنَّ الصحابة رضي الله عنهم كانوا مجمعين على وقوع الرؤية في الآخرة، قال الإمام مالك رضي الله تعالى عنه : لما حجب أعداؤه فلم يروه تجلّى لأوليائه حتى رأوه، ولو لم ير المؤمنون ربهم يوم القيامة لم يُعير الكافرون بالحجاب، قال تعالى: ﴿كَلَّا إِنَّهُمْ عَن رَّبِّهِمْ يَوْمَئِذٍ مَحْجُوبُونَ﴾ [المطففين: ١٥]. وقال الإمام الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه : لمَّا حَجب قوماً بالسُّخط، دلَّ على أن
As for the Sunnah, there are hadiths such as the hadith: 'You will surely see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon.' The comparison is for seeing in terms of absence of doubt and hiddenness, not for the seen as might be imagined. The expression with the letter sin (sa) in the hadith is because the Hereafter is near. The Mu'tazila interpreted the hadith as meaning: you will see the mercy of your Lord. As for consensus, it is that the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, were unanimously agreed upon the occurrence of vision in the afterlife. Imam Malik, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'When He veiled His enemies so they did not see Him, He manifested to His friends until they saw Him. If the believers did not see their Lord on the Day of Resurrection, the disbelievers would not be reproached with the veil.' Allah said: 'No! Indeed, from their Lord that day they will be veiled' (Mutaffifin 15). Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'When He veiled a people with displeasure, it indicates that
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents the proofs from Sunnah and consensus (ijma') for the vision of Allah. First, it cites the famous hadith comparing seeing Allah to seeing the full moon—clarifying that the comparison is in clarity and certainty, not in the nature of the seen. The use of the future tense (sīn) indicates the nearness of the Hereafter. The Mu'tazila are refuted for interpreting the hadith as seeing Allah's mercy, not Himself. Then the passage cites the consensus of the Companions on ru'ya in the afterlife. It quotes Imam Malik's reasoning: since Allah veils the disbelievers, it implies the believers see Him. The passage also cites the Quranic verse that mentions the veil for disbelievers. Finally, it begins quoting Imam al-Shafi'i's argument that veiling with displeasure indicates that seeing follows pleasure. The quote is incomplete but reinforces the point. This lesson solidifies the doctrinal proof from tradition and collective authority.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الحديث
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith implies that Allah is similar to the moon in appearance.
Correction: The comparison is only about the clarity and certainty of the vision, not about the nature of the seen.

Checkpoint
How did the Mu'tazila reinterpret the hadith 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon'?
A) They said it means seeing the moon
B) They said it means seeing Allah's mercy
C) They said it means seeing on the Day of Judgment
D) They said it means seeing the Prophet

Key Takeaway
The hadith proof for ru'ya is preserved by clarifying that the comparison is about certainty, not about Allah's likeness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that you will see Allah with certainty, or do you harbor doubts that your faith is only in the unseen and not a future vision? What would change in your daily life if you were absolutely certain of this sight?
?
When you read the hadith comparing seeing Allah to the moon, did you ever mentally picture Allah in a form? How can you train yourself to focus on the certainty of the vision rather than imagining the seen?
?
The Mu'tazila reinterpreted the clear hadith to fit their theology. Are there any teachings in your own understanding that you are tempted to reinterpret to avoid the weight of their apparent meaning? Will you submit to the clear text even if it challenges your intellect?

Consensus and Arguments from the Veil
Proof from ijma' and statements of Malik and Shafi'i for ru'ya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus of the Companions and the reasoning of Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i support the belief in seeing Allah.

Core Idea
If Allah veils disbelievers, it proves believers see Him.

Beyond the hadith, the consensus of the Companions forms a strong proof for ru'ya. Imams Malik and Shafi'i used the Quranic verse about veiling to argue that seeing Allah is reserved for believers in the afterlife. This lesson presents their reasoning and the authoritative basis of ijma'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the consensus claim
The passage asserts that the Companions were unanimous on the occurrence of vision in the afterlife. Consider the authority of ijma'.
Ijma' is a primary source of Islamic law and theology.
2
Examine Imam Malik's argument
Imam Malik argued from the veiling of disbelievers: because Allah veils them, it implies believers see Him. Read his statement carefully.
He uses the Quranic verse from Surat al-Mutaffifin as support.
3
Note Imam al-Shafi'i's argument
Imam al-Shafi'i is quoted as saying: 'When He veiled a people with displeasure, it indicates that...' Reflect on how this completes the logic.
The passage is cut off, but the idea is that veiling with displeasure implies that seeing is a reward for pleasure.
